</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a></li>
</ul>

<td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:United_States_space_program_sidebar" title="Template:United States space program sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:United_States_space_program_sidebar" title="Template talk:United States space program sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:United_States_space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Skylab</b> was the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>' first <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbiting" title="Orbiting">orbiting</a> <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> from 1973 to 1979, when it fell back to Earth amid huge worldwide media attention. Launched and operated by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, Skylab included a workshop, a solar observatory, and other systems necessary for crew survival and scientific experiments. It was launched unmanned by a modified <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket, with a weight of 170,000 pounds (77,111 kg). Lifting Skylab into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_earth_orbit" title="Low earth orbit">low earth orbit</a> was the final mission and launch of a Saturn V rocket (which was famous for carrying the <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing#Manned_Moon_landings" title="Moon landing">manned Moon landing missions</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1973_Evaluation_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-1973_Evaluation-2">[2]</a></sup> Skylab was not simply a place of habitation; massive science experimentation was undertaken there. When data from these experiments (some of which were on physical film) were returned to Earth, analysis of scientific and engineering data of each mission was completed. Skylab's solar observatory was one of its major functions. Solar science was significantly advanced by the telescope, and its observation of the Sun was unprecedented. As the Skylab program drew to a close, NASA's focus had shifted to the development of the Space Shuttle, through which NASA hoped to reduce the cost of space access compared to previous launch systems.</p>
<p>To transport <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronauts" title="Astronauts">astronauts</a> to Skylab, there were a total of three manned expeditions to the station, conducted between May 1973 and February 1974. Each of these missions delivered a three-astronaut crew, carried in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">Apollo Command/Service Module</a> (Apollo CSM) launched atop the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a> rocket, which is much smaller than the Saturn V. For the final two manned missions to Skylab, a backup Apollo CSM/Saturn IB was assembled and made ready in case an in-orbit rescue mission was needed, but this backup vehicle was never flown.</p>
<p>The station was damaged during launch when the <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">micrometeoroid</a> shield separated from the workshop and tore away, taking one of the main <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panel</a> arrays with it and jamming the other main solar panel array so that it could not deploy. This deprived Skylab of most of its electrical power, and also removed protection from intense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">solar heating</a>, threatening to make it unusable. However, the first crew was able to save Skylab by deploying a replacement heat shade and freeing the jammed solar panels. This was the first time a repair of this magnitude was performed in space.</p>
<p>Skylab included the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">Apollo Telescope Mount</a> (a multi-spectral <a href="/wiki/Solar_observatory" title="Solar observatory">solar observatory</a>), Multiple Docking Adapter (with two docking ports), <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">Airlock</a> Module with <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA) hatches, and the Orbital Workshop (the main habitable space inside Skylab). Electrical power came from solar arrays, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_cells" title="Fuel cells">fuel cells</a> in the docked Apollo CSM. The rear of the station included a large waste tank, propellant tanks for maneuvering jets, and a heat radiator.</p>
<p>Numerous scientific experiments were conducted aboard Skylab during its operational life, and crews were able to confirm the existence of <a href="/wiki/Coronal_hole" title="Coronal hole">coronal holes</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. The Earth Resources Experiment Package (EREP) was used to view Earth with sensors that recorded data in the visible, infrared, and microwave spectral regions. Thousands of photographs of Earth were taken, and the record for human time spent in orbit was extended beyond the 23 days set by the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_11" title="Soyuz 11">Soyuz 11</a> crew aboard <a href="/wiki/Salyut_1" title="Salyut 1">Salyut 1</a>, to as much as 84 days by the <a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">Skylab 4</a> crew. Plans were made to refurbish and reuse Skylab by using the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> to boost its orbit and repair it. However, due to delays with the development of the Space Shuttle, Skylab's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decaying_orbit" title="Decaying orbit">decaying orbit</a> could not be stopped.</p>
<p>In the hours before re-entry, NASA <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller" title="Flight controller">ground controllers</a> attempted to adjust Skylab's trajectory and orientation to try to minimize the risk of debris landing in populated areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-3">[3]</a></sup> NASA's attempted target was a spot 810 miles (1,300 km) south-southeast of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. Skylab's <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric reentry</a> began on July 11, 1979,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-4">[4]</a></sup> and people on earth and an airline pilot saw dozens of colorful firework-like flares as large pieces of the space station broke up in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> Skylab did not burn up as fast as NASA expected, and Skylab debris landed southeast of <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>, resulting in a debris path between <a href="/wiki/Esperance,_Western_Australia" title="Esperance, Western Australia">Esperance</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rawlinna" title="Rawlinna">Rawlinna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-4">[4]</a></sup> Over a single property in Esperance, 24 pieces of Skylab were found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> Analysis of some debris indicated that the Skylab station had disintegrated 10 mi (16 km) above the Earth, much lower than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Skylab, NASA space station/laboratory projects included <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuttle-Mir" title="Shuttle-Mir">Shuttle-<i>Mir</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Freedom" title="Space Station Freedom">Space Station <i>Freedom</i></a> (which was later merged into the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rocket <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a> <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, and other early advocates of manned space travel, expected until the 1960s that a space station would be an important early step in space exploration. Von Braun participated in the publishing of a series of influential articles in <i><a href="/wiki/Collier%27s" title="Collier's">Collier's</a></i> magazine from 1952 to 1954, titled "<a href="/wiki/Man_Will_Conquer_Space_Soon!" title="Man Will Conquer Space Soon!">Man Will Conquer Space Soon!</a>". He envisioned a large, circular station 250 feet (75m) in diameter that would rotate to generate <a href="/wiki/Artificial_gravity" title="Artificial gravity">artificial gravity</a> and require a fleet of 7,000-ton (6,500-metric ton) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle" title="Space shuttle">space shuttles</a> for construction in orbit. The 80 men aboard the station would include astronomers operating a telescope, meteorologists to forecast the weather, and soldiers to conduct surveillance. Von Braun expected that future expeditions to the Moon and Mars would leave from the station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a>, led in the 1950s and early 1960s to unmanned satellites that could take photographs of weather patterns or enemy nuclear weapons and send them to Earth. A large station was no longer necessary for such purposes, and the United States <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> to send men to the Moon chose a mission mode that would not need in-orbit assembly. A smaller station that a single rocket could launch retained value, however, for scientific purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_studies">Early studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1959, von Braun, head of the Development Operations Division at the <a href="/wiki/Army_Ballistic_Missile_Agency" title="Army Ballistic Missile Agency">Army Ballistic Missile Agency</a>, submitted his final <a href="/wiki/Project_Horizon" title="Project Horizon">Project Horizon</a> plans to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a>. The overall goal of Horizon was to place men on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, a mission that would soon be taken over by the rapidly forming NASA. Although concentrating on the Moon missions, von Braun also detailed an orbiting laboratory built out of a Horizon upper stage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> an idea used for Skylab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> A number of NASA centers studied various space station designs in the early 1960s. Studies generally looked at platforms launched by the Saturn V, followed up by crews launched on Saturn IB using an <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">Apollo Command/Service Module</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Gemini capsule</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> on a <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Titan II-C</a>, the latter being much less expensive in the case where cargo was not needed. Proposals ranged from an Apollo-based station with two to three men, or a small "canister" for four men with Gemini capsules resupplying it, to a large, rotating station with 24 men and an operating lifetime of about five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> A proposal to study the use of a Saturn <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> as a manned space laboratory was documented in 1962 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft" title="Douglas Aircraft">Douglas Aircraft</a> Company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_Force_plans">Air Force plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Air Force plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOL_USAF.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="551" data-file-width="695" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/MOL_USAF.png/220px-MOL_USAF.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/MOL_USAF.png/330px-MOL_USAF.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/MOL_USAF.png/440px-MOL_USAF.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOL_USAF.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration for MOL</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> (DoD) and NASA cooperated closely in many areas of space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In September 1963, NASA and the DoD agreed to cooperate in building a space station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-15">[15]</a></sup> The DoD wanted its own manned facility, however,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heppenheimer203_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heppenheimer203-16">[16]</a></sup> and in December it announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_Orbital_Laboratory" title="Manned Orbital Laboratory">Manned Orbital Laboratory</a> (MOL), a small space station primarily intended for photo reconnaissance using large telescopes directed by a two-man crew. The station was the same diameter as a <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Titan II</a> upper stage, and would be launched with the crew riding atop in a modified Gemini capsule with a hatch cut into the <a href="/wiki/Heat_shield" title="Heat shield">heat shield</a> on the bottom of the capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> MOL competed for funding with a NASA station for the next five years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and politicians and other officials often suggested that NASA participate in MOL or use the DoD design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heppenheimer203_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heppenheimer203-16">[16]</a></sup> The military project led to changes to the NASA plans so that they would resemble MOL less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="720" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg/220px-Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg/330px-Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg/440px-Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assembling_the_Skylab_Orbital_Workshop_7014162.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The floor grating of Skylab under construction</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo_Applications_Program">Apollo Applications Program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Apollo Applications Program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Program" title="Apollo Applications Program">Apollo Applications Program</a></div>
<p>NASA management was concerned about losing the 400,000 workers involved in Apollo after landing on the Moon in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A reason von Braun, head of NASA's <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> during the 1960s, advocated for a smaller station after his large one was not built was that he wished to provide his employees with work beyond developing the Saturn rockets, which would be completed relatively early during Project Apollo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> NASA set up the <i>Apollo Logistic Support System Office</i>, originally intended to study various ways to modify the Apollo hardware for scientific missions. The office initially proposed a number of projects for direct scientific study, including an extended-stay lunar mission which required two Saturn V launchers, a "lunar truck" based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Module" title="Lunar Module">Lunar Module</a> (LEM), a large manned solar telescope using a LEM as its crew quarters, and small space stations using a variety of LEM or CSM-based hardware. Although it did not look at the space station specifically, over the next two years the office would become increasingly dedicated to this role. In August 1965, the office was renamed, becoming the <i>Apollo Applications Program</i> (AAP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>As part of their general work, in August 1964 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Manned Spacecraft Center</a> (MSC) presented studies on an expendable lab known as <i>Apollo "X"</i>, short for <i>Apollo Extension System</i>. "Apollo X" would have replaced the LEM carried on the top of the <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> stage with a small space station slightly larger than the CSM's service area, containing supplies and experiments for missions between 15 and 45 days' duration. Using this study as a baseline, a number of different mission profiles were looked at over the next six months.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wet_workshop">Wet workshop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wet workshop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wet_workshop" title="Wet workshop">Wet workshop</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2327" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg/170px-Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg/255px-Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg/340px-Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._von_Braun%27s_Sketch_of_the_Space_Station_8883912_original.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Von Braun's sketch of a Space Station based on conversion of a Saturn V stage, 1964.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In November 1964, von Braun proposed a more ambitious plan to build a much larger station built from the <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> second stage of a Saturn V. His design replaced the S-IVB third stage with an aeroshell, primarily as an adapter for the CSM on top. Inside the shell was a 10-foot (3.0 m) cylindrical equipment section. On reaching orbit, the <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> second stage would be vented to remove any remaining hydrogen fuel, then the equipment section would be slid into it via a large inspection hatch. This became known as a "wet workshop" concept, because of the conversion of an active fuel tank. The station filled the entire interior of the S-II stage's hydrogen tank, with the equipment section forming a "spine" and living quarters located between it and the walls of the booster. This would have resulted in a very large 33-by-45-foot (10.1 by 13.7 m) living area. Power was to be provided by <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> lining the outside of the S-II stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>One problem with this proposal was that it required a dedicated Saturn V launch to fly the station. At the time the design was being proposed, it was not known how many of the then-contracted Saturn Vs would be required to achieve a successful Moon landing. However, several planned Earth-orbit test missions for the LEM and CSM had been canceled, leaving a number of Saturn IBs free for use. Further work led to the idea of building a smaller "wet workshop" based on the S-IVB, launched as the second stage of a Saturn IB.</p>
<p>A number of S-IVB-based stations were studied at MSC from mid-1965, which had much in common with the Skylab design that eventually flew. An <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a> would be attached to the hydrogen tank, in the area designed to hold the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">LEM</a>, and a minimum amount of equipment would be installed in the tank itself in order to avoid taking up too much fuel volume. Floors of the station would be made from an open metal framework that allowed the fuel to flow through it. After launch, a follow-up mission launched by a Saturn IB would launch additional equipment, including solar panels, an equipment section and docking adapter, and various experiments. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft" title="Douglas Aircraft">Douglas Aircraft</a>, builder of the S-IVB stage, was asked to prepare proposals along these lines. The company had for several years been proposing stations based on the S-IV stage, before it was replaced by the S-IVB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wet_Workshop.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="711" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Wet_Workshop.svg/220px-Wet_Workshop.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Wet_Workshop.svg/330px-Wet_Workshop.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Wet_Workshop.svg/440px-Wet_Workshop.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wet_Workshop.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early "<a href="/wiki/Wet_workshop" title="Wet workshop">wet workshop</a>" version of Skylab</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 1, 1966, MSC sent out contracts to <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft_Company" title="Douglas Aircraft Company">Douglas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grumman" title="Grumman">Grumman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell</a> for the conversion of a S-IVB spent stage, under the name <i>Saturn S-IVB spent-stage experiment support module</i> (SSESM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In May, astronauts voiced concerns over the purging of the stage's hydrogen tank in space. Nevertheless, in late July it was announced that the Orbital Workshop would be launched as a part of Apollo mission AS-209, originally one of the Earth-orbit CSM test launches, followed by two Saturn I/CSM crew launches, AAP-1 and AAP-2.</p>
<p>MOL remained AAP's chief competitor for funds, although the two programs cooperated on technology. NASA considered flying experiments on MOL, or using its <a href="/wiki/Titan_IIIC" title="Titan IIIC">Titan IIIC</a> booster instead of the much more expensive Saturn IB. The agency decided that the Air Force station was not large enough, and that converting Apollo hardware for use with Titan would be too slow and too expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The DoD later canceled MOL in June 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dry_workshop">Dry workshop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dry workshop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Design work continued over the next two years, in an era of shrinking budgets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mc670127_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-mc670127-29">[29]</a></sup> (NASA sought $450 million for Apollo Applications in fiscal year 1967, for example, but received $42 million.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In August 1967, the agency announced that the lunar mapping and base construction missions examined by the AAP were being canceled. Only the Earth-orbiting missions remained, namely the Orbital Workshop and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">Apollo Telescope Mount</a> solar observatory.</p>
<p>The success of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> in December 1968, launched on the third flight of a Saturn V, made it likely that one would be available to launch a dry workshop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Later, several Moon missions were canceled as well, originally to be <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Apollo_missions" title="Canceled Apollo missions">Apollo missions 18 through 20</a>. The cancellation of these missions freed up three Saturn V boosters for the AAP program. Although this would have allowed them to develop von Braun's original S-II based mission, by this time so much work had been done on the S-IV based design that work continued on this baseline. With the extra power available, the wet workshop was no longer needed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> the S-IC and S-II lower stages could launch a "dry workshop", with its interior already prepared, directly into orbit.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitability">Habitability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Habitability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="843" data-file-width="782" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff/lossy-page1-300px-SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff/lossy-page1-450px-SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff/lossy-page1-600px-SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SkyLab_Isometric_2.tiff" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Labeled cut-away view of the main workshop</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A dry workshop simplified plans for the interior of the station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Industrial_design" title="Industrial design">Industrial design</a> firm <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Loewy" title="Raymond Loewy">Raymond Loewy</a>/William Snaith recommended emphasizing <a href="/wiki/Habitability" title="Habitability">habitability</a> and comfort for the astronauts by, for example, providing a <a href="/wiki/Wardroom" title="Wardroom">wardroom</a> for meals and relaxation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and a window to view Earth and space, although astronauts who participated in Skylab planning were dubious about the designers' focus on areas such as <a href="/wiki/Color_scheme" title="Color scheme">color schemes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-35">[35]</a></sup> Habitability had not previously been an area of concern when building spacecraft, due to their small volume and brief mission durations, but the Skylab missions would last for months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> NASA sent a scientist on <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Piccard#Ben_Franklin_mission" title="Jacques Piccard">Jacques Piccard's <i>Ben Franklin</i> submarine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> in July and August 1969, to learn how six people would live in an enclosed space for four weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Astronauts were uninterested in watching movies on a proposed <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_center" title="Entertainment center">entertainment center</a> or playing games, but did want books and individual music choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-35">[35]</a></sup> Food was also important; early Apollo crews complained about its quality, and a NASA volunteer found living on the Apollo food for four days on Earth to be intolerable; its taste and composition, in the form of cubes and squeeze tubes, were unpleasant. <a href="/wiki/Space_food#Skylab_.281973-1974.29" title="Space food">Skylab food</a> significantly improved on its predecessors by prioritizing edibility over scientific needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each astronaut had a private sleeping area the size of a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walk-in_closet" title="Walk-in closet">walk-in closet</a>, with a curtain, <a href="/wiki/Sleeping_bag" title="Sleeping bag">sleeping bag</a>, and locker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Designers also added a shower<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvp.7CBelew.7C1977.7Cp.3D80.7Cps_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvp.7CBelew.7C1977.7Cp.3D80.7Cps-41">[41]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Space_toilet" title="Space toilet">toilet</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> the latter was both for comfort and to obtain precise urine and feces samples for examination on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Waste was put in a large waste tank that occupied a large section of below the OWS. Although Skylab did not have a system to recycle urine into drinking water for example, it also did not dump trash into space to dispose of it. Trash and waste water were put in the trash airlock, which injected it into the big waste tank. Its contents re-entered Earth's atmosphere with the rest of Skylab in 1979.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rescue">Rescue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rescue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skylab_Rescue" title="Skylab Rescue">Skylab Rescue</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Skylab_Rescue" title="Skylab Rescue">Rescuing astronauts from Skylab</a> was possible in the most likely emergency circumstances. The crew could use the CSM to quickly return to Earth if the station suffered serious damage. If the CSM failed, the spacecraft and Saturn IB for the next Skylab mission would have been launched with two astronauts to retrieve the crew; given Skylab's ample supplies, its residents would have been able to wait up to several weeks for the rescue mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ep-107_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ep-107-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_history">Operational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Operational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Completion_and_launch">Completion and launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Completion and launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3921" data-file-width="5678" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Skylab_launch.jpg/300px-Skylab_launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Skylab_launch.jpg/450px-Skylab_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Skylab_launch.jpg/600px-Skylab_launch.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Launch of the modified <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket carrying the Skylab space station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On August 8, 1969, the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas" title="McDonnell Douglas">McDonnell Douglas</a> Corporation received a contract for the conversion of two existing S-IVB stages to the Orbital Workshop configuration. One of the S-IV test stages was shipped to McDonnell Douglas for the construction of a mock-up in January 1970. The Orbital Workshop was renamed "Skylab" in February 1970 as a result of a NASA contest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The actual stage that flew was the upper stage of the AS-212 rocket (the S-IVB stage, S-IVB 212). The mission computer used aboard Skylab was the <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/System_4_Pi" title="System 4 Pi">System/4Pi</a> TC-1, a relative of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AP-101" title="AP-101">AP-101</a> Space Shuttle computers. A Saturn V originally produced for the Apollo program—before the cancellation of Apollo 18, 19, and 20—was repurposed and redesigned to launch Skylab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The Saturn V's upper stage was removed, but with the controlling <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Instrument Unit</a> remaining in its standard position.</p>
<p>Skylab was launched on May 14, 1973 by the modified Saturn V. The launch is sometimes referred to as Skylab 1, or SL-1. Severe damage was sustained during launch and deployment, including the loss of the station's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor" title="Meteor">micrometeoroid</a> shield/sun shade and one of its main <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a>. Debris from the lost micrometeoroid shield further complicated matters by pinning the remaining solar panel to the side of the station, preventing its deployment and thus leaving the station with a huge power deficit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Immediately following Skylab's launch, Pad A at <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39" title="Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39">Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39</a> was deactivated, and construction proceeded to modify it for the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> program, originally targeting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancelled_Space_Shuttle_missions#STS-1A" title="Cancelled Space Shuttle missions">a maiden launch in March 1979</a>. The manned missions to Skylab would occur using a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a> rocket from Launch Pad 39B.</p>
<p>SL-1 would be the final unmanned launch from LC-39 until February 19, 2017, when <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-10" title="SpaceX CRS-10">SpaceX CRS-10</a> was launched.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_missions">Manned missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Manned missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7973" data-file-width="5444" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg/220px-S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg/330px-S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg/440px-S73-32568skylab3saturnibnight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3900" data-file-width="3820" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg/220px-Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg/330px-Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg/440px-Skylab_3_flyaround.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2340" data-file-width="3000" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg/220px-False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg/330px-False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg/440px-False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:False_Color_Isophote_of_a_Solar_Eruption.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Solar prominence recorded by Skylab on August 21, 1973<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Three manned missions, <a href="#Mission_designations">designated</a> <a href="/wiki/Skylab_2" title="Skylab 2">SL-2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skylab_3" title="Skylab 3">SL-3</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">SL-4</a>, were made to Skylab. The first manned mission, SL-2, launched on May 25, 1973 atop a Saturn IB and involved extensive repairs to the station. The crew deployed a parasol-like sunshade through a small instrument port from the inside of the station bringing station temperatures down to acceptable levels and preventing overheating that would have melted the plastic insulation inside the station and released poisonous gases. This solution was designed by NASA's "Mr. Fix It" <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kinzler" title="Jack Kinzler">Jack Kinzler</a>, who won the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Distinguished_Service_Medal" title="NASA Distinguished Service Medal">NASA Distinguished Service Medal</a> for his efforts. The crew conducted further repairs via two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacewalk" title="Spacewalk">spacewalks</a> (extra-vehicular activity, or EVA). The crew stayed in orbit with Skylab for 28 days. Two additional missions followed, with the launch dates of July 28, 1973 (SL-3) and November 16, 1973 (SL-4), and mission durations of 59 and 84 days, respectively. The last Skylab crew returned to Earth on February 8, 1974.</p>
<p>In addition to the three manned missions, there was a Rescue mission on standby that had a crew of two, but could take 5 back down.</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Skylab 2</i>: launched May 25, 1973</li>
<li><i>Skylab 3</i>: launched July 28, 1973</li>
<li><i>Skylab 4</i>: launched November 16, 1973</li>
<li><i>Skylab 5</i>: cancelled</li>
<li><i>Skylab Rescue</i> on standby</li>
</ul>
<p>Also of note was the three-man crew of <i>Skylab Medical Experiment Altitude Test</i>, who spent 56 days at low-pressure in 1972 on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.nasa.gov_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-history.nasa.gov-52">[52]</a></sup> This was a spaceflight analog test in full gravity, but various Skylab hardware and medical knowledge was gained</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_operations">Orbital operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Orbital operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Skylab orbited Earth 2,476 times during the 171 days and 13 hours of its occupation during the three manned Skylab expeditions. Each of these extended the human record of 23 days for amount of time spent in space set by the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_11" title="Soyuz 11">Soyuz 11</a> crew aboard the space station <a href="/wiki/Salyut_1" title="Salyut 1">Salyut 1</a> on June 30, 1971. <a href="/wiki/Skylab_2" title="Skylab 2">Skylab 2</a> lasted 28 days, <a href="/wiki/Skylab_3" title="Skylab 3">Skylab 3</a> 56 days, and <a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">Skylab 4</a> 84 days. Astronauts performed ten spacewalks, totaling 42 hours and 16 minutes. Skylab logged about 2,000 hours of scientific and medical experiments, 127,000 frames of film of the Sun and 46,000 of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Solar experiments included photographs of eight <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a>, and produced valuable results<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> that scientists stated would have been impossible to obtain with unmanned spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The existence of the Sun's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronal_holes" title="Coronal holes">coronal holes</a> were confirmed because of these efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Many of the experiments conducted investigated the astronauts' adaptation to extended periods of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microgravity" title="Microgravity">microgravity</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2939" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/220px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/330px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/440px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Owen_Garriott" title="Owen Garriott">Owen Garriott</a> performing an EVA in 1973</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A typical day began at 6 AM <a href="/wiki/Central_Time_Zone" title="Central Time Zone">Central Time Zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Although the toilet was small and noisy, both veteran astronauts—who had endured earlier missions' rudimentary waste-collection systems—and rookies complimented it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvp.7CBelew.7C1977.7Cp.3D80.7Cps_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-harvp.7CBelew.7C1977.7Cp.3D80.7Cps-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19730625_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-time19730625-59">[59]</a></sup> The first crew enjoyed taking a shower once a week, but found drying themselves in weightlessness<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19730625_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-time19730625-59">[59]</a></sup> and vacuuming excess water difficult; later crews usually cleaned themselves daily with wet washcloths instead of using the shower. Astronauts also found that bending over in weightlessness to put on socks or tie shoelaces strained their stomach muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Breakfast began at 7 AM. Astronauts usually stood to eat, as sitting in microgravity also strained their stomach muscles. They reported that their food—although greatly improved from Apollo—was bland and repetitive, and weightlessness caused utensils, food containers, and bits of food to float away; also, gas in their drinking water contributed to <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a>. After breakfast and preparation for lunch, experiments, tests and repairs of spacecraft systems and, if possible, 90 minutes of physical exercise followed; the station had a bicycle and other equipment, and astronauts could jog around the water tank. After dinner, which was scheduled for 6 PM, crews performed household chores and prepared for the next day's experiments. Following lengthy daily instructions (some of which were up to 15 meters long) sent via <a href="/wiki/Teleprinter" title="Teleprinter">teleprinter</a>, the crews were often busy enough to postpone sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The station offered what a later study called "a highly satisfactory living and working environment for crews", with enough room for personal privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Although it had a <a href="/wiki/Darts" title="Darts">dart set</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darts_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-darts-64">[64]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Playing_cards" title="Playing cards">playing cards</a>, and other recreational equipment in addition to books and music players, the window with its view of Earth became the most popular way to relax in orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Experiments">Experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The figure lists an overview of most major experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Skylab 4 carried out several more experiments, such as to observe <a href="/wiki/Comet_Kohoutek" title="Comet Kohoutek">Comet Kohoutek</a>.</p>
