<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_vaults_and_window_radiation_shield">Film vaults and window radiation shield</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Film vaults and window radiation shield">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P188skylabfilmvault.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="542" data-file-width="781" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/P188skylabfilmvault.jpg/220px-P188skylabfilmvault.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/P188skylabfilmvault.jpg/330px-P188skylabfilmvault.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/P188skylabfilmvault.jpg/440px-P188skylabfilmvault.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P188skylabfilmvault.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A labeled illustration of a Skylab film vault, from <i>Skylab: A Guidebook</i> (EP-107) by NASA</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Skylab had certain features to protect vulnerable technology from radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-67">[67]</a></sup> The window was vulnerable to darkening, and this darkening could affect experiment S190.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-67">[67]</a></sup> As a result, a light shield that could be open or shut was designed and installed on Skylab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-67">[67]</a></sup> To protect a wide variety of films, used for a variety of experiments and for astronaut photography, there were 5 film vaults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov1_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov1-68">[68]</a></sup> There were four smaller film vaults in the Multiple Docking Adapter, which had to have four not one, because each spar could not carry enough weight for single larger film vault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov1_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov1-68">[68]</a></sup> The orbital workshop could handle a single larger safe, which is also more efficient for shielding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov1_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov1-68">[68]</a></sup> The large vault in the orbital workshop had an empty mass of 2398 lb (1088 kg, 171.3 stones).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov1_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov1-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-67">[67]</a></sup> The four smaller vaults had combined mass of 1545 lb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov1_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov1-68">[68]</a></sup> The primary construction material of all five safes was aluminum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov1_68-5"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov1-68">[68]</a></sup> When Skylab re-entered there was one 180 lb chunk of aluminum found that was thought to be a door to one of the film vaults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The big film vault was one of the heaviest single pieces of Skylab to re-enter Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>A later example of a radiation vault is the <a href="/wiki/Juno_Radiation_Vault" title="Juno Radiation Vault">Juno Radiation Vault</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a> orbiter for Jupiter; in that case it was designed to protect much of the unmanned spacecraft's electronics and it used 1 cm thick walls of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film vault was used for storing film from various sources including the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">Apollo Telescope Mount</a> solar instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72">[72]</a></sup> Six ATM experiments used film to record data, and over the course of the missions over 150, 000 successful exposures were recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72">[72]</a></sup> The film canister had to be manually retrieved on manned spacewalks to the instruments during the missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72">[72]</a></sup> The film canisters were returned to Earth aboard the Apollo capsules when each mission ended, and were among the heaviest items that had to be returned at the end of each mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov2-73">[73]</a></sup> The heaviest canisters weighed 40 kg and could hold up to 16,000 frames of film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov2_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov2-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gyroscopes">Gyroscopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Gyroscopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P46bskylabgyros.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="617" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/P46bskylabgyros.jpg/220px-P46bskylabgyros.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/P46bskylabgyros.jpg/330px-P46bskylabgyros.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/P46bskylabgyros.jpg/440px-P46bskylabgyros.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P46bskylabgyros.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skylab could change its attitude without using propellant by changing the spin of large gyroscopes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were two types of gyroscopes on Skylab. Control-moment gyroscopes could physically move the station, and rate gyroscopes measured the rate of rotation to find its orientation. (see <a href="/wiki/Control_moment_gyroscope" title="Control moment gyroscope">Control moment gyroscope</a> (CMG))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The CMG helped provide the fine pointing needed by the Apollo Telescope Mount, and to resist various forces that can change the station's orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov3_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov3-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the forces acting on Skylab that the pointing system needed to resist:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov3_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov3-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gravity-gradient_stabilization" title="Gravity-gradient stabilization">gravity gradient</a></li>
<li>aerodynamic disturbance</li>
<li>internal movements of crew</li>
</ul>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Skylab-A attitude and pointing control system has been developed to meet the high accuracy requirements established by the desired experiment conditions. Conditions must be maintained by the control system under the influence of external and internal disturbance torques, such as gravity gradient and aerodynamic disturbances and onboard astronaut motion.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i>SKYLAB ATTITUDE AND POINTING CONTROL SYSTEM</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov3_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov3-75">[75]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Skylab was one of the first large spacecraft to use big <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a>, that could control its attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov4_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov4-76">[76]</a></sup> The control could also be used to help point the instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov4_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov4-76">[76]</a></sup> The gyroscopes took about ten hours to get spun up if they were turned off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov5_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov5-77">[77]</a></sup> There was also a thruster system to control Skylab's attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov5_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov5-77">[77]</a></sup> There were 9 rate-gyroscope sensors, 3 for each axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov5_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov5-77">[77]</a></sup> These were sensors that fed their output to the Skylab digital computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov5_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov5-77">[77]</a></sup> Two of three were active and their input was averaged, while the third was a backup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov5_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov5-77">[77]</a></sup> From NASA SP-400 <i>Skylab, Our First Space Station</i>, "each Skylab control-moment gyroscope consisted of a motor-driven rotor, electronics assembly, and power inverter assembly. The 21-inch diameter rotor weighed 155 pounds and rotated at approximately 8950 revolutions per minute".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov6_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov6-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were three control movement gyroscopes on Skylab, but only two were required to maintain pointing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov6_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov6-78">[78]</a></sup> The control and sensor gyroscopes were part of a system that help detect and control the orientation of the station in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov6_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov6-78">[78]</a></sup> Other sensors that helped with this were a Sun tracker and a star tracker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov6_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov6-78">[78]</a></sup> The sensors fed data to the main computer, which could then use the control gyroscopes and or the thruster system to keep Skylab pointed as desired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov6_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov6-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shower">Shower</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Shower">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1977" data-file-width="2934" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg/220px-Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg/330px-Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg/440px-Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Showering_on_Skylab_-_GPN-2000-001710.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Astronaut in the shower, with curtain partially down. July 1973</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="630" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg/220px-Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg/330px-Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg/440px-Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_2_Conrad_in_shower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Conrad in the Skylab shower in 1973</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P268skylabshowertest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="222" data-file-width="440" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/07/P268skylabshowertest.jpg/220px-P268skylabshowertest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/07/P268skylabshowertest.jpg/330px-P268skylabshowertest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/07/P268skylabshowertest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P268skylabshowertest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Earth testing showing partially and fully enclosed positions of the shower "curtain"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Skylab had a zero-gravity shower system in the work and experiment section of the Orbital Workshop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-79">[79]</a></sup> The shower that had a cylindrical curtain that went from the floor to ceiling, and inside water was dispensed at the push of a button from a nozzle at the end of a flexible hose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-space.com-80">[80]</a></sup> In addition there was a vacuum system to suck away water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-space.com-80">[80]</a></sup> The floor of the shower (in the usual orientation of this module) had foot restraints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-79">[79]</a></sup> The user had to bring a bottle of pressurized water, which was dispensed through the flexible hose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-79">[79]</a></sup> Liquid soap was used with this shower, and the use of both this soap and the water for showers were carefully planned out as with most items on the station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-79">[79]</a></sup> The first astronaut to use the space shower was <a href="/wiki/Paul_J._Weitz" title="Paul J. Weitz">Paul J. Weitz</a> (On <a href="/wiki/Skylab_2" title="Skylab 2">Skylab 2</a>, the first manned mission )<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-79">[79]</a></sup> The system was designed for up to 6 pints (3/4 of a gallon or about 2.84 liters) of water per shower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarity_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarity-81">[81]</a></sup> The water was dispensed from a push button nozzle attached to flexible hose, which took water from the personal hygiene tank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part3b_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-part3b-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>The shower curtain had several hoop rings and soft fabric to contain the water and/or soap that may float in the lack of gravity, which would be sucked up by a vacuum system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarity_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clarity-81">[81]</a></sup> Skylab also supplied astronauts with rayon terrycloth towels, which had a color-coded stitching for each crew-member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov7_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov7-83">[83]</a></sup> In all there were 420 towels on board Skylab initially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-79">[79]</a></sup> The normal allotment was that there was enough liquid soap and warm water for one shower per week per person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"..it took a fair amount longer to use than you might expect, but you come out smelling good."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Astronaut Weitz as quoted in SP-400 Skylab, Our First Space Station<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov7_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov7-83">[83]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In the 1980s there was a plan to remove the Skylab B to prepare for future space station plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarity_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clarity-81">[81]</a></sup> Scientists would spend 9 months testing and trying to improve the design for the future space station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarity_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clarity-81">[81]</a></sup> It was possible to remove it from the National Air and Space museum and use it, since it was a functional flight spare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarity_81-4"><a href="#cite_note-Clarity-81">[81]</a></sup> As it turned out, the <a href="/wiki/Habitation_Module" title="Habitation Module">Habitation Module</a> was not deployed for what morphed into the International Space Station.</p>
<p>Inhabitants of the Shuttle did not have an enclosed shower, they had to take sponge baths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-85">[85]</a></sup> Similarly though there was device to dispense water, even hot water, and a vacuuming system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-85">[85]</a></sup> One of the big concerns with bathing in space is that the small droplets of water do not cause an electrical short by floating into the wrong area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-85">[85]</a></sup> Like the rest of the interior, especially the crew areas were designed by NASA scientists and engineers, but also with input from famed industrial designer <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Loewy" title="Raymond Loewy">Raymond Loewy</a>, who was employed as a Habitability Consultant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Procedure for operating the shower:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacearch_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-spacearch-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Fill up the pressurized portable water bottle with hot water and attach that bottle to ceiling</li>
<li>Connect the hose and pull up the shower curtain</li>
<li>Spray down with water</li>
<li>Apply liquid soap and spray more water</li>
<li>Vacuum up all the fluids and stow items</li>
</ol>
<p>From start to finish a Skylab shower would take about 2 and half hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guastello_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guastello-89">[89]</a></sup> One aspect was not just taking the shower but closing it up afterwards, because it was unwise to leave water droplets floating around.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guastello_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guastello-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The vacuum water system fed to a centrifugal separator, filter, and collection bag to allow the system to vacuum up the fluids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacearch_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-spacearch-88">[88]</a></sup> Waste water would be injected into a disposal bag which would in turn be put in the waste tank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.nasa.gov_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-history.nasa.gov-52">[52]</a></sup> The fabric material for the shower enclosure was fire-proof <a href="/wiki/Beta_cloth" title="Beta cloth">beta cloth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part3b_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-part3b-82">[82]</a></sup> The fabric was wrapped around hoops of 109 cm diameter, the top hoop was connected to the ceiling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part3b_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-part3b-82">[82]</a></sup> One of the suggestions was for the nozzle of the suction head to be more flexible, increasing its utitlity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacearch_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-spacearch-88">[88]</a></sup> The hose for the spray head connected at the top of the shower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacearch_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-spacearch-88">[88]</a></sup> The shower could be collapsed down to the ground when not in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacearch_88-4"><a href="#cite_note-spacearch-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>A simulated Skylab shower was also used during the 56-day Earth bound Skylab analog mission SMEAT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shayler_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shayler-90">[90]</a></sup> The report was that they though the in-orbit they thought the astronauts would find it a positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shayler_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shayler-90">[90]</a></sup> During SMEAT the astronauts used the shower after heavy exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shayler_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shayler-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Skylab shower was designed and built at the Manned Spaceflight Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part3b_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-part3b-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cameras_and_film">Cameras and film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cameras and film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="2370" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg/280px-Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg/420px-Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg/560px-Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_3_Close-Up_-_GPN-2000-001711.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A view of the Skylab space station taken with a hand-held 70 mm Hasselblad camera using a 100 mm lens and SO-368 medium speed Ektachrome film</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4248" data-file-width="4318" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg/300px-Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg/450px-Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg/600px-Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sl2-05-364crete1973.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> as photographed on June 22, 1973 from Skylab</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:40_Years_Ago,_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1235" data-file-width="2700" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg/280px-40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg/420px-40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg/560px-40_Years_Ago%2C_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:40_Years_Ago,_Skylab_Paved_Way_for_International_Space_Station.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skylab as SL2 mission departs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There was a variety of hand-held and fixed experiments that used various types of film. In addition to the instruments in the ATM solar observatory, there was 35 and 70 mm film cameras. There was also a TV camera on board, this recorded an image electronically.</p>
<p>It was determined that film was predicted to fog up to due to radiation over the course of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-67">[67]</a></sup> To counter-act this the film was stored in one of several different vaults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Personal (hand-held) camera equipment:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Television camera
<ul>
<li>Westinghouse color</li>
<li>25-150mm zoom</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/16_mm_film" title="16 mm film">16 mm film</a> video camera (Maurer), called the 16 mm Data Acquisition Camera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-91">[91]</a></sup> The DAC was capable of very low frame rates, such as for engineering data films, and it had independent shutter speeds and framing rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-92">[92]</a></sup> It could be powered from a battery or from Skylab itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-92">[92]</a></sup> It could handle interchangeable lenses, and various lens and also film types were provided during the missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-92">[92]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>There was different options for <a href="/wiki/Frame_rate" title="Frame rate">frame rates</a>: 2, 4, 6, 12 and 24 frames per second<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>It was the Maurer 16 mm DAC</li>
<li>Available lenses for the film-video camera 5, 10, 18, 25, 75, &amp; 100 mm lenses</li>
<li>Films for 16 mm DAC
<ul>
<li>Ektachrome film</li>
<li>SO-368 film</li>
<li>SO-168 film</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Film for the DAC were contained in DAC Film Magazine, which contained up to 140 feet (42.7 m) of this base 16 mm film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> At 24 framers per second this was enough for 4 minutes of filming, with progressively longer film times with lower frame rates such as 16 minutes at 6 frames per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-92">[92]</a></sup> The film had to be loaded or unloaded from the DAC in a photographic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_room" title="Dark room">dark room</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>35 mm film camera (Nikon)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov8_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov8-95">[95]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>There were 5 Nikon 35 mm film cameras on board, including 55 mm and 300 mm lenses for the camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>It was a specially modified <a href="/wiki/Nikon_F" title="Nikon F">Nikon F</a> camera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imaging.nikon.com_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-imaging.nikon.com-97">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li>The camera was capable of interchangeable lenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imaging.nikon.com_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-imaging.nikon.com-97">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li>35mm films included:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ektachrome" title="Ektachrome">Ektachrome</a></li>
<li>SO-368,</li>
<li>SO-168</li>
<li>2485 type film</li>
<li>2443 type film</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>70 mm film camera (Hasselblad)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-91">[91]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>This had an electric data camera system with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reseau_plate" title="Reseau plate">Reseau plate</a></li>
<li>Films included
<ul>
<li>70 mm Ektachrome</li>
<li>SO-368 film</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Lenses: 70 mm lens, 100 mm lens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>There was also a pair of Leitz Trinovid 10 x 40 Binoculars modified for use in space to aid in Earth observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov8_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov8-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additional cameras:</p>
<p>Experiment S190B was the Actron Earth Terrain Camera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov8_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov8-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>S190A was <i>Multispectral Photographic Camera</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch5b_91-4"><a href="#cite_note-ch5b-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>This consisted of six cameras 70 mm cameras</li>
<li>Each was an Itek 70 mm boresighted camera</li>
<li>Lens was a f/2.8 with a 21.2 degree <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plans_for_re-use_after_the_last_mission">Plans for re-use after the last mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Plans for re-use after the last mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SkylabRescue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2940" data-file-width="2363" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/SkylabRescue.jpg/170px-SkylabRescue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/SkylabRescue.jpg/255px-SkylabRescue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/SkylabRescue.jpg/340px-SkylabRescue.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SkylabRescue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skylab Rescue vehicle Apollo CSM being removed from its <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a> rocket after the last Skylab misson</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The three crewed Skylab missions used only about 16.8 of the 24 man-months of oxygen, food, water, and other supplies stored aboard Skylab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> A fourth manned mission was under consideration, which would have used the launch vehicle kept on standby for the Skylab Rescue mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com-100">[100]</a></sup> This would have been a 20-day mission to boost Skylab to a higher altitude and do more scientific experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com-100">[100]</a></sup> Another plan was to use a <a href="/wiki/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System" title="Teleoperator Retrieval System">Teleoperator Retrieval System</a> (TRS) launched aboard the Space Shuttle (then under development), to robotically re-boost the orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-101">[101]</a></sup> When Skylab 5 was cancelled, it was expected Skylab would stay in orbit until the 1980s, which enough time to overlap with the beginning of Shuttle launches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-101">[101]</a></sup> Other options for launching TRS included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_III" title="Titan III">Titan III</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_Agena" title="Atlas Agena">Atlas Agena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-101">[101]</a></sup> None of these options received the level of effort and funding needed for execution before Skylab's sooner-than-expected re-entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-101">[101]</a></sup> Skylab's internal systems were evaluated and tested from the ground, and effort was put into plans for re-using it, as late as 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though no one returned after the end of the SL-4 mission in February 1974, the crew left a bag filled with supplies to welcome visitors, and left the hatch unlocked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-103">[103]</a></sup> NASA discouraged any discussion of additional visits due to the station's age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> but in 1977 and 1978, when the agency still believed the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> would be ready by 1979, it completed two studies on reusing the station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> By September 1978, the agency believed Skylab was safe for crews, with all major systems intact and operational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> It still had 180 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man-days" title="Man-days">man-days</a> of water and 420 man-days of oxygen, and astronauts could refill both;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-103">[103]</a></sup> the station could hold up to about 600 to 700 man-days of drinkable water and 420 man-days of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Before SL-4 left they did one more boost, running the Skylab thrusters for 3 minutes which added 11 km in height to its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-108">[108]</a></sup> Skylab was left in a 433 by 455 km orbit on departure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-108">[108]</a></sup> At this time, the NASA-accepted estimate for its re-entry was nine years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>..Calculations made during the mission, based on current values for solar activity and expected atmospheric density, gave the workshop just over nine years in orbit. Slowly at first-dropping 30 kilometers by 1980-and then faster-another 100 kilometers by the end of 1982-Skylab would come down, and some time around March 1983 it would burn up in the dense atmosphere.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i>SP-4208 Living and Working In Space: A History of Skylab</i>, Chapter 19<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov9_108-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov9-108">[108]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The studies cited several benefits from reusing Skylab, which one called a resource worth "hundreds of millions of dollars"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMB1-13_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMB1-13-109">[109]</a></sup> with "unique habitability provisions for long duration space flight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-110">[110]</a></sup> Because no more operational Saturn V rockets were available after the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, four to five shuttle flights and extensive <a href="/wiki/Space_architecture" title="Space architecture">space architecture</a> would have been needed to build another station as large as Skylab's 12,400 cubic feet (350 m<sup>3</sup>) volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Its ample size—much greater than that of the shuttle alone, or even the shuttle plus <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>—was enough, with some modifications, for up to seven astronauts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> of both sexes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and experiments needing a long duration in space;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMB1-13_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMB1-13-109">[109]</a></sup> even a movie projector for recreation was possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Proponents of Skylab's reuse also said repairing and upgrading Skylab would provide information on the results of long-duration exposure to space for future stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-103">[103]</a></sup> The most serious issue for reactivation was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stationkeeping" title="Stationkeeping">stationkeeping</a>, as one of the station's <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a> had failed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson361_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-benson361-115">[115]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attitude_control_system" title="Attitude control system">attitude control system</a> needed refueling; these issues would need EVA to fix or replace. The station had not been designed for extensive resupply. However, although it was originally planned that Skylab crews would only perform limited maintenance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> they successfully made major repairs during EVA, such as the SL-2 crew's deployment of the solar panel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and the SL-4 crew's repair of the primary coolant loop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The SL-2 crew fixed one item during EVA by, reportedly, "hit[ting] it with [a] hammer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some studies also said, beyond the opportunity for space construction and maintenance experience, reactivating the station would free up shuttle flights for other uses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMB1-13_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMB1-13-109">[109]</a></sup> and reduce the need to <a href="/wiki/Extended_Duration_Orbiter" title="Extended Duration Orbiter">modify the shuttle for long-duration missions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Even if the station were not manned again, went one argument, it would serve as a useful experimental platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shuttle_mission_plans">Shuttle mission plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Shuttle mission plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3552" data-file-width="4620" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg/220px-Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg/330px-Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg/440px-Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Teleoperator_Retrieval_System_with_Shuttle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Concept for proposed Skylab re-boost</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The reactivation would likely have occurred in four phases:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_103-4"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li><a href="/wiki/Canceled_Space_Shuttle_missions#STS-2A_.28Columbia.29" title="Canceled Space Shuttle missions">An early Space Shuttle flight</a> would have boosted Skylab to a higher orbit, adding five years of operational life. The shuttle might have pushed or towed the station, but attaching a booster—the <a href="/wiki/Teleoperator_Retrieval_System" title="Teleoperator Retrieval System">Teleoperator Retrieval System</a> (TRS)—to the station would have been more likely, based on astronauts' training for the task. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Marietta" title="Martin Marietta">Martin Marietta</a> won the contract for $26 million to design the apparatus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790101_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-time19790101-124">[124]</a></sup> TRS would contain about three tons of propellant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The remote-controlled booster had TV cameras and was designed for duties such as space construction and servicing and retrieving satellites the shuttle could not reach. After rescuing Skylab, the TRS would have remained in orbit for future use. Alternatively, it could have been used to de-orbit Skylab for a safe, controlled re-entry and destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dempewolff197808_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-dempewolff197808-126">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li>In two shuttle flights, Skylab would have been refurbished. In January 1982, the first mission would have attached a docking adapter and conducted repairs. In August 1983, a second crew would have replaced several system components.</li>
<li>In March 1984, shuttle crews would have attached a solar-powered Power Expansion Package, refurbished scientific equipment, and conducted 30- to 90-day missions using the Apollo Telescope Mount and the earth resources experiments.</li>
<li>Over five years, Skylab would have been expanded to accommodate six to eight astronauts, with a new large docking/interface module, additional logistics modules, Spacelab modules and pallets, and an orbital vehicle space dock using the shuttle's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/External_tank" title="External tank">external tank</a>.</li>
</ol>
<p>The first three phases would have required about $60 million in 1980s dollars, not including launch costs.</p>
<p>Other options for launching TRS were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_III" title="Titan III">Titan III</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_Agena" title="Atlas Agena">Atlas Agena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix.com1_101-4"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix.com1-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="After_departure">After departure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: After departure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_4_undocking.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3917" data-file-width="3917" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Skylab_4_undocking.jpg/220px-Skylab_4_undocking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Skylab_4_undocking.jpg/330px-Skylab_4_undocking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Skylab_4_undocking.jpg/440px-Skylab_4_undocking.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_4_undocking.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skylab in February 1974, as SL-4 departs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After a boost of 6.8 miles (10.9 km) by SL-4's Apollo CSM before its departure in 1974, Skylab was left in a <a href="/wiki/Parking_orbit" title="Parking orbit">parking orbit</a> of 269 miles (433 km) by 283 miles (455 km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson361_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-benson361-115">[115]</a></sup> that was expected to last until at least the early 1980s, based on estimates of the 11-year <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspot_cycle" title="Sunspot cycle">sunspot cycle</a> that began in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-128">[128]</a></sup> NASA first considered as early as 1962 the potential risks of a space station reentry, but decided not to incorporate a <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">retrorocket</a> system in Skylab due to cost and acceptable risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The spent 49-ton <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> stage which had launched Skylab in 1973 remained in orbit for almost two years, and made an uncontrolled reentry on January 11, 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Some debris, most prominently the five heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J-2_(rocket_engine)" title="J-2 (rocket engine)">J-2</a> engines, likely survived to impact in the North Atlantic Ocean. Although this event did not receive heavy media or public attention, it was followed closely by NASA and the Air Force, and helped emphasize the need for improved planning and public awareness for Skylab's eventual reentry.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_activity">Solar activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Solar activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2361" data-file-width="3000" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg/220px-Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg/330px-Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg/440px-Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_Solar_flare.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skylab captured this view of the Sun</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>British mathematician <a href="/wiki/Desmond_King-Hele" title="Desmond King-Hele">Desmond King-Hele</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">Royal Aircraft Establishment</a> predicted in 1973 that Skylab would de-orbit and crash to earth in 1979, sooner than NASA's forecast, because of increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-128">[128]</a></sup> Greater-than-expected solar activity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> heated the outer layers of Earth's atmosphere and increased drag on Skylab. By late 1977, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NORAD" title="NORAD">NORAD</a> also forecast a reentry in mid-1979;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (NOAA) scientist criticized NASA for using an inaccurate model for the second most-intense sunspot cycle in a century, and for ignoring NOAA predictions published in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reentry of the USSR's nuclear powered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmos_954" title="Cosmos 954">Cosmos 954</a> in January 1978, and the resulting radioactive debris fall in northern Canada, drew more attention to Skylab's orbit. Although Skylab did not contain radioactive materials, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a> warned NASA about the potential diplomatic repercussions of station debris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Battelle_Memorial_Institute" title="Battelle Memorial Institute">Battelle Memorial Institute</a> forecast that up to 25 tons of metal debris could land in 500 pieces over an area 4,000 miles long and 1,000 miles wide. The lead-lined film vault, for example, might land intact at 400 feet per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ground controllers re-established contact with Skylab in March 1978<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> and recharged its batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-3">[3]</a></sup> Although NASA worked on plans to reboost Skylab with the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> through 1978 and the TRS was almost complete, the agency gave up in December when it became clear that the shuttle would not be ready in time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790101_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-time19790101-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> its first flight, <a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a>, did not occur until April 1981. Also rejected were proposals to launch the TRS using one or two unmanned rockets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oberg199202_103-5"><a href="#cite_note-oberg199202-103">[103]</a></sup> or to attempt to destroy the station with missiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-entry">Re-entry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Re-entry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_reentry_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="512" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Skylab_reentry_map.svg/220px-Skylab_reentry_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Skylab_reentry_map.svg/330px-Skylab_reentry_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Skylab_reentry_map.svg/440px-Skylab_reentry_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_reentry_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Equirectangular_projection" title="Equirectangular projection">Equirectangular projection</a> relief map of the Skylab reentry site and final orbits as predicted by NASA</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylabfragment.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="543" data-file-width="692" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Skylabfragment.JPG/220px-Skylabfragment.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Skylabfragment.JPG/330px-Skylabfragment.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Skylabfragment.JPG/440px-Skylabfragment.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylabfragment.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fragment of Skylab recovered after its re-entry through Earth's atmosphere, on display at the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Skylab's demise was an international media event, with merchandising of T-shirts and hats with <a href="/wiki/Bullseye_(target)" title="Bullseye (target)">bullseyes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> and "Skylab Repellant" with a money-back guarantee,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrier19790708_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-carrier19790708-137">[137]</a></sup> wagering on the time and place of re-entry, and nightly news reports. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Examiner" title="San Francisco Examiner">San Francisco Examiner</a></i> offered a $10,000 prize for the first piece of Skylab delivered to its offices; the competing <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">Chronicle</a></i> offered $200,000 if a subscriber suffered personal or property damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-3">[3]</a></sup> A Nebraska neighborhood painted a target so the station would have "something to aim for", a resident said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrier19790708_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-carrier19790708-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>NASA calculated that the odds of station re-entry debris hitting any human were 1 to 152, which when multiplied by 4 billion becomes 1 in 600 billion for a specific human,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> although the odds of debris hitting a city of 100,000 or more were 1 to 7 and special teams were readied to head to any country hit by debris and requesting help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-3">[3]</a></sup> The event caused so much panic in the Philippines that then <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Philippines" title="President of the Philippines">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> appeared on national television to reassure the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>"We assume that Skylab is on the planet Earth, somewhere."</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Charles S. Harlan, Skylab mission controller<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>A week before re-entry, NASA forecast that it would occur between July 10 to 14, with the 12th the most likely date, and the Royal Aircraft Establishment predicted the 14th.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19790704_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-ap19790704-128">[128]</a></sup> In the hours before the event, ground controllers adjusted Skylab's orientation to try to minimize the risk of re-entry on a populated area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time19790716_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-time19790716-3">[3]</a></sup> They aimed the station at a spot 810 miles (1,300 km) south southeast of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, South Africa, and re-entry began at approximately 16:37 UTC, July 11, 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-4">[4]</a></sup> The Air Force provided data from a secret tracking system able to monitor the reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindoh_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindoh-139">[139]</a></sup> The station did not burn up as fast as NASA expected. Due to a 4% calculation error, debris landed about 300 miles (480 km) southeast of <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-4">[4]</a></sup> and was found between <a href="/wiki/Esperance,_Western_Australia" title="Esperance, Western Australia">Esperance</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rawlinna" title="Rawlinna">Rawlinna</a>, from 31° to 34°S and 122° to 126°E, about 130–150 km (81–93 miles) radius around <a href="/wiki/Balladonia,_Western_Australia" title="Balladonia, Western Australia">Balladonia</a>. Residents and an airline pilot saw dozens of colorful fireworks-like flares as large pieces broke up in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shire_of_Esperance" title="Shire of Esperance">Shire of Esperance</a> facetiously fined NASA <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$</a>400 for littering, a fine which remained unpaid for 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The fine was paid in April 2009, when radio show host Scott Barley of Highway Radio raised the funds from his morning show listeners and paid the fine on behalf of NASA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seventeen-year-old Stan Thornton found 24 pieces of Skylab at his home in Esperance. A <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> businessman flew him, his parents, and his girlfriend to <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, where he collected the <i>Examiner</i> prize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson371_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-benson371-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup> In a coincidence for the organizers, the annual <a href="/wiki/Miss_Universe_1979" title="Miss Universe 1979">Miss Universe</a> pageant was scheduled to be held a few days later, on July 20, 1979 in Perth. A large piece of Skylab debris was displayed on the stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Analysis of the debris showed that the station had disintegrated 10 miles (16 km) above the Earth, much lower than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1984_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1984-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the demise of Skylab, NASA focused on the reusable <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a> module, an orbital workshop that could be deployed with the Space Shuttle and returned to Earth. The next American major space station project was <a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Freedom" title="Space Station Freedom">Space Station <i>Freedom</i></a>, which was merged into the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> in 1993, and launched starting in 1998. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuttle-Mir" title="Shuttle-Mir">Shuttle-<i>Mir</i></a> was another project, and led to the U.S. funding <a href="/wiki/Spektr" title="Spektr">Spektr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priroda" title="Priroda">Priroda</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mir_Docking_Module" title="Mir Docking Module"><i>Mir</i> Docking Module</a> in the 1990s.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_reentering_space_debris" title="List of reentering space debris">List of reentering space debris</a></div>
<h2><span id="Rockets,_rescue,_and_cancelled_missions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rockets.2C_rescue.2C_and_cancelled_missions">Rockets, rescue, and cancelled missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Rockets, rescue, and cancelled missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2877" data-file-width="4187" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg/220px-Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg/330px-Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg/440px-Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist_concept_illustrating_of_Skylab_Rescue_Command_Module.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
5-person Apollo Command module for the Apollo Rescue mission, could take 2 up and 5 down</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg/220px-Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg/330px-Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg/440px-Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Space_Center_-_Rocket_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
SA-209 served on standby for Skylab 4 and ASTP, and has been preserved at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> rocket garden.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There was a <a href="/wiki/Skylab_Rescue" title="Skylab Rescue">Skylab Rescue</a> mission assembled for the second manned mission to Skylab, but it was not needed. Another rescue mission was assembled for the last Skylab and was also on standby for ASTP. That launch stack might have been used for Skylab 5, what would be the fourth manned Skylab mission but this was cancelled and the SA-209 Saturn IB rocket was put on display at NASA <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historicspacecraft.com_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-historicspacecraft.com-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Launch vehicles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historicspacecraft.com_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-historicspacecraft.com-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>SA-206 (Skylab 2)</li>
<li>SA-207 (Skylab 3)</li>
<li>SA-208 (Skylab 4)</li>
<li>SA-209 (Skylab Rescue, not-launched)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab_5">Skylab 5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Skylab 5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Skylab 5 would have been a short 20-day mission to conduct more scientific experiments and use the Apollo's Service Propulsion System engine to boost Skylab into a higher orbit. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vance_Brand" title="Vance Brand">Vance Brand</a> (commander), <a href="/wiki/William_B._Lenoir" title="William B. Lenoir">William B. Lenoir</a> (science pilot), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_Lind" title="Don Lind">Don Lind</a> (pilot) would have been the crew for this mission, with Brand and Lind being the prime crew for the Skylab Rescue flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautixskylab5_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautixskylab5-145">[145]</a></sup> Brand and Lind also trained for a mission that would have aimed Skylab for a controlled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deorbit" title="Deorbit">deorbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindoh_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-lindoh-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab_B">Skylab B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Skylab B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skylab_B" title="Skylab B">Skylab B</a></div>
<p>In addition to the flown Skylab space station, a second flight-quality backup Skylab space station had been built during the program. NASA considered using it for a second station in May 1973 or later, to be called Skylab B (S-IVB 515), but decided against it. Launching another Skylab with another Saturn V rocket would have been very costly, and it was decided to spend this money on the development of the Space Shuttle instead. The backup is on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_mock-ups">Engineering mock-ups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Engineering mock-ups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A full-size training mock-up once used for astronaut training is located at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center</a> visitor's center in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>. Another full-size training mock-up is at the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>. Originally displayed indoors, it was subsequently stored outdoors for several years to make room for other exhibits. To mark the 40th anniversary of the Skylab program, the Orbital Workshop portion of the trainer was restored and moved into the Davidson Center in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> NASA transferred <a href="/wiki/Skylab_B" title="Skylab B">the backup Skylab</a> to the National Air and Space Museum in 1975. On display in the Museum's Space Hall since 1976, the orbital workshop has been slightly modified to permit viewers to walk through the living quarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_designations">Mission designations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Mission designations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The numerical identification of the manned Skylab missions was the cause of some confusion. Originally, the unmanned launch of Skylab and the three manned missions to the station were numbered <i>SL-1</i> through <i>SL-4</i>. During the preparations for the manned missions, some documentation was created with a different scheme -- <i>SLM-1</i> through <i>SLM-3</i>—for those missions only. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Pogue" title="William Pogue">William Pogue</a> credits <a href="/wiki/Pete_Conrad" title="Pete Conrad">Pete Conrad</a> with asking the Skylab program director which scheme should be used for the <a href="/wiki/Mission_patch" title="Mission patch">mission patches</a>, and the astronauts were told to use 1-2-3, not 2-3-4. By the time NASA administrators tried to reverse this decision, it was too late, as all the in-flight clothing had already been manufactured and shipped with the 1-2-3 mission patches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>

<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">Skylab 4 <i>SL-4</i> (<i>SLM-3</i>)</a></th>

<dl>
<dt>SMEAT</dt>
</dl>
<p>The <i>Skylab Medical Experiment Altitude Test</i> or SMEAT was a 56-day (8 week) Earth analog Skylab test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The test had a low-pressure high oxygen-percentage atmosphere but had full gravity due to the effect of being on Earth's surface. The test had a three-man crew with Commander (Crippen), Science Pilot (Bobko), and Pilot (Thornton);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> there was a focus on medical studies and Thornton was an M.D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> They entered a pressure chamber converted to be more a like Skylab on July 26, 1972, and it ended on September 20, 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.nasa.gov_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-history.nasa.gov-52">[52]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_cost">Program cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skylab&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Program cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 1966 to 1974, the Skylab program cost a total of $2.2 billion, equivalent to $10 billion in 2010 dollars. As its three three-man crews spent 510 total man-days in space, each man-day cost approximately $20 million, compared to $7.5 million for the International Space Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lafleur20100308_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-lafleur20100308-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
